<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1190595574">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1190595574" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7553206-paul-nelson" class="left imgcol" title="Paul Nelson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1434048386p2/7553206.jpg" alt="Paul Nelson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1190595574?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 03, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7553206-paul-nelson" class="user" title="Paul Nelson">Paul Nelson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Paperback 9780140283334" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7553206-paul-nelson?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7553206-paul-nelson?shelf=2015-books-read" class="actionLinkLite">2015-books-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1190595574">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15243330461745000177">Lord of the Flies is of course about a group of English schoolboys that survive a plane crash and get stranded on an island. At its core the book is about the innocence of youth and its development when they are forced inadvertently to mimic certain cultures of human society just to survive, as they edge ever closer toward destruction. All the key players even though they are children show a stereotypical persona that can be compared easily within various societies throughout history.<br /><br />You have a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15243330461745000177">Lord of the Flies is of course about a group of English schoolboys that survive a plane crash and get stranded on an island. At its core the book is about the innocence of youth and its development when they are forced inadvertently to mimic certain cultures of human society just to survive, as they edge ever closer toward destruction. All the key players even though they are children show a stereotypical persona that can be compared easily within various societies throughout history.<br /> <br />You have a good intentioned leader who necessitates the need for a structured environment and is able to ask for advice but hasn't got the power or leadership skills to maintain control when adversity strikes. Ralph.<br /> <br />You have an isolated boy who has led a sheltered life, the modern day nerd who had medical problems that prevented him joining in with the other boys. In this day and age the perfect target for bullies. His situation kind of represents factions of mankind not being allowed involvement in the islands or looking deeply, the world's decision making processes highlighted by dependence on his glasses to see. Piggy.<br /> <br />You have the young man who was initially quiet but when war broke out became the epitome of violence and the chief enforcer to the tyrant. Roger.<br /> <br />Finally the reckless tyrant, charismatic but lacking foresight. The boys see his personality and bravado, and he becomes the most natural leader to follow. His superficial method of running the show is quick decision time followed by terror to enforce his way when decisions backfire. Hiding behind his war paint, his actions of setting the island on fire perfectly represent the dictator ruling with violence. Jack.<br /> <br />The competition for power and rule is commonplace through history and is something present all through the rise of mankind and civilisation in various forms. Leadership and rebellion, disagreement is a right, murder is not and the line between right and wrong fades very quickly. This story is used in comparison to various Asian countries and cultures through history but it's an easy message or moral to understand. Themes of savagery versus civilisation and fascism versus democracy, and the two sided coin of morality.<br /> <br />You could call Lord of the Flies a somewhat harsh blueprint to humanity and it’s a grim statement of human behaviour from the ground up. Its message will stand through history but whereas some stories are timeless, this didn't feel that way for me. The writing style feels as though it's from a long time ago, as of course it is, some classics are that powerful you feel as though they could have been written at any time. So this is a poignant message and it’s read for the morals that jump from the page but to be honest it wasn’t enjoyable and it shows its age.<br /> <br />I think my next school project type read will be either 1984 or A Clockwork Orange.<br /><br />Also posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://paulnelson.booklikes.com/post/1283138/lord-of-the-flies-by-william-golding" target="_blank">http://paulnelson.booklikes.com/post/...</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15243330461745000177">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_220537724">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/220537724" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450116936p2/6313408.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/220537724?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 22, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Paperback 9780140283334" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=classic-literature" class="actionLinkLite">classic-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die" class="actionLinkLite">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer220537724">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1448930442733715514"><br />I remain convinced to this day that <em>The Lord of the Flies</em> as a controversial classic is one of those books that depends upon how you read it. I think that at the surface it appears to be a text which is simple and a little dull. When I read it back in 2007 or so I found it incredibly dull. The richness and life I saw in other classics were not apparent. However now that I think back and reflect upon this novel I see it as a grand story, one that extends beyond whatever the perversities of the au</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1448930442733715514"><br />I remain convinced to this day that <em>The Lord of the Flies</em> as a controversial classic is one of those books that depends upon how you read it. I think that at the surface it appears to be a text which is simple and a little dull. When I read it back in 2007 or so I found it incredibly dull. The richness and life I saw in other classics were not apparent. However now that I think back and reflect upon this novel I see it as a grand story, one that extends beyond whatever the perversities of the author may have been.<br /><br />On the surface it could be read as a simple moralistic tale - a portrayal of what happens when you take people away from society. However I view the book differently. I don't think of it as a portrayal of what happens when individuals are separated from society. I think it is a portrayal of what exists deep down within societies and the propensity towards evil. My particular world view sees the world is both simple and complex (a mix of wonder and logic if you will). People are both good and evil mixed together, by which I mean people are, in my view, as capable of good as they are of evil (at least our human vision of good and evil). To sum down humanity into science, chemicals and hormones is something I disagree with and I'm certain many scientific theories exist about whether such things like <em>Lord of the Flies</em> could ever happen. I have no doubt that they could because the mind is such a complex device, a mortal engine that with the right conditioning could snap. I believe however that people don't want to think about it and don't want to reflect on what perhaps they could be capable of.<br /><br />My favourite way to read and think about <em>Lord of the Flies</em> is as allegory. The boys representing 'sinful' man, that particular mixture of good and evil (which I perceive as more subtle than we realise - if we were to look at the world from a more eternal perspective would it not be a simple 'evil' even to ignore the homeless person asking for food? Perhaps we commit a lot more evil than we realise, even if it is out of ignorance.) And the ending of the book of course in a Christian sense represents the return of true order.<br /><br />Lord of the Flies is an impossible book to rate from the point of view of enjoyment. There is simply little within it to enjoy. There is however hidden depth and meaning that create a dilemma for the reviewer. Despite me being unable to like the plot I can appreciate the brilliance of how Golding explores the reality of the darkness of men's hearts. And how he subtly alludes to the Christ story through his dark themes reveals his incredible insight. Therefore despite finding the tale itself gruesome I must give this novel five stars for its ability to unnerve and evoke questions of what is the true darkness behind men. Are we really all simply captives of the Lord of the Flies awaiting a saving sailor? </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1448930442733715514">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1410990751">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6812221253461518785">Some books are required reading for good reason. A book like 'Lord of the Flies' is what I like to think of more as 'demanded reading'. <br /><br />There are a handful of works in the world that absolutely need to be read by all, and you can't call yourself an avid reader if you've missed out on them. William Golding's masterpiece is one such book. <br /><br />'Lord of the Flies' is not so much a classic as it is a pillar in the literary Parthenon. It ain't just for high school curriculum, it's for every literate perso</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6812221253461518785">Some books are required reading for good reason. A book like 'Lord of the Flies' is what I like to think of more as 'demanded reading'. <br /><br />There are a handful of works in the world that absolutely need to be read by all, and you can't call yourself an avid reader if you've missed out on them. William Golding's masterpiece is one such book. <br /><br />'Lord of the Flies' is not so much a classic as it is a pillar in the literary Parthenon. It ain't just for high school curriculum, it's for every literate person to experience, absorb, and ponder. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1460075488">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1460075488" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/49652450-sabah" class="left imgcol" title="Sabah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451687005p2/49652450.jpg" alt="Sabah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1460075488?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/49652450-sabah" class="user" title="Sabah">Sabah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Paperback 9780140283334" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1460075488">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2927457112074441938">I read this as a child not once but several times, often making my petrifying journey through its pages twice a year. I still have my original copy with foot notes in the margin and yes, I'm sorry to say, the definitions of words I often struggled to comprehend, but regardless of whom I soldiered on.<br /><br />Lord of the Flies was written by William Golding in 1954. It centres around a group of boys who find themselves stranded on a deserted island after their plane is shot down. No adults survive the cra</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2927457112074441938">I read this as a child not once but several times, often making my petrifying journey through its pages twice a year. I still have my original copy with foot notes in the margin and yes, I'm sorry to say, the definitions of words I often struggled to comprehend, but regardless of whom I soldiered on.<br /><br />Lord of the Flies was written by William Golding in 1954. It centres around a group of boys who find themselves stranded on a deserted island after their plane is shot down. No adults survive the crash and so the boys are left to fend for themselves. At the outset they try desperately to establish civility and order within their new found paradise, happy to be free of adult intervention and are successful for a period. This success is, however, short-lived as slowly the group, much like savage animals, turn in on each other. What follows is a horrifying indictment of man's loss of humanity, accountability and civility. How easily and swiftly, in a need to survive, we can be reduced to nothing more than savage, barbaric beasts with the thin veneer of civilisation no longer present to cloak our baser characteristics.<br /><br />Lord of the flies is a superb novel. Goldings' use of children was a very deliberate decision and I was left questioning, very much as I'm sure he intended, if children, who we consider to be pure and innocent can convert and commit themselves to such barbarity then to what extent will adults go?<br /><br />He is also brutal in a holding mirror for us to reveal a realistic perspective of the human race and shows with clarity of purpose the evil to which we may all be prone to when all remnants of moral propriety are removed.<br /><br />Goldings use of symbolism is done so subtly that you only become aware after a 2nd or 3rd read which is why I have gladly revisited this book so often and each time I have been left reeling with a new realisation.<br /><br />This is without doubt a darkly dystopian adventure that may leave you divided, for Golding is brutal in his telling of the truth of man's capacity for evil. Regardless of whether you love it or loath it I can definitely guarantee that it will leave you struggling to answer with Goldings' level of honesty , what would you be willing to sacrifice in order to survive?<br /><br />If you enjoy this novel as much as I did then I can heartily recommend A Brave New World by Aldous Huxley. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2927457112074441938">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a href="/review/show/79290077?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 06, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2990642-rowena" class="user" title="Rowena">Rowena</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer79290077">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8156669266590769277">3.5 stars <br /><br />I haven't read this book since I was 11 years old. It disturbed me then, it disturbs me even more now. I guess with my Sociology background it was quite fascinating to see how societal roles were created among the boys. Thoughts that came to mind were do we naturally desire control by authority, and do we feel more comfortable with rules being in place? As one of the children said, " Because the rules are the only thing we've got." <br /><br />I remember my English teacher telling my class not to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8156669266590769277">3.5 stars <br /><br />I haven't read this book since I was 11 years old. It disturbed me then, it disturbs me even more now. I guess with my Sociology background it was quite fascinating to see how societal roles were created among the boys. Thoughts that came to mind were do we naturally desire control by authority, and do we feel more comfortable with rules being in place? As one of the children said, " Because the rules are the only thing we've got." <br /><br />I remember my English teacher telling my class not to be too shocked about the going-ons in the book as they were the natural progression of things. I'm not too sure if that's always the case but it is an interesting thought (and one nobody in my class agreed with at the time).<br /><br />Side note: those kids survived pretty well on the island. I wonder how kids these days would survive under the same conditions.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8156669266590769277">...more</a>

          </span>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_16113'), "\n\n  &lt;h2&gt;&lt;a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16113.A_Dance_to_the_Music_of_Time?from_choice=false&amp;amp;from_home_module=false\"&gt;A Dance to the Music of Time: 1st Movement&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\n\n      &lt;div&gt;\n        by &lt;a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/9947.Anthony_Powell?a=7&amp;amp;origin=related_works\"&gt;Anthony Powell&lt;\/a&gt;\n      &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n          &lt;div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\"&gt;\n            &lt;span class=\"minirating\"&gt;&lt;span class=\"stars staticStars\"&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"&gt;&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;\/span&gt; 3.94 avg rating &amp;mdash; 3,010 ratings&lt;\/span&gt;            &amp;mdash; published 1951\n          &lt;\/div&gt;\n\n    &lt;div class=\"addBookTipDescription\"&gt;\n      \n&lt;span id=\"freeTextContainer17138198829548048025\"&gt;Anthony Powell\'s universally acclaimed epic encompasses a four-volume panorama of twentieth century London. Hailed by Time as \"brilliant literary comedy as well as a brilliant sketch of the times,\" A Dance to the Music of Time opens just after World&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText17138198829548048025\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;Anthony Powell\'s universally acclaimed epic encompasses a four-volume panorama of twentieth century London. Hailed by Time as &amp;quot;brilliant literary comedy as well as a brilliant sketch of the times,&amp;quot; A Dance to the Music of Time opens just after World War I. Amid the fever of the 1920s and the first chill of the 1930s, Nick Jenkins and his friends confront sex, society, business, and art. In the second volume they move to London in a whirl of marriage and adulteries, fashions and frivolities, personal triumphs and failures. These books &amp;quot;provide an unsurpassed picture, at once gay and melancholy, of social and artistic life in Britain between the wars&amp;quot; (Arthur Schlesinger, Jr.). The third volume follows Nick into army life and evokes London during the blitz. In the climactic final volume, England has won the war and must now count the losses.\n\nFour very different young men on the threshold of manhood dominate this opening volume of A Dance to the Music of Time. The narrator, Jenkins—a budding writer—shares a room with Templer, already a passionate womanizer, and Stringham, aristocratic and reckless. Widermerpool, as hopelessly awkward as he is intensely ambitious, lurks on the periphery of their world. Amid the fever of the 1920s and the first chill of the 1930s, these four gain their initiations into sex, society, business, and art. Considered a masterpiece of modern fiction, Powell\'s epic creates a rich panorama of life in England between the wars.\n\nIncludes these novels: \nA Question of Upbringing \nA Buyer\'s Market \nThe He then sets out to prove his theory by murdering a vile, cynical old pawnbroker and her sister. The act brings Raskolnikov into contact with his own buried conscience and with two characters — the deeply religious Sonia, who has endured great suffering, and Porfiry, the intelligent and discerning official who is charged with investigating the murder — both of whom compel Raskolnikov to feel the split in his nature. For the next twenty-two years, Tita and Pedro are forced to circle each other in unconsummated passion.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/306.William_Golding">About William Golding</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/306.William_Golding"><img alt="306" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1198342496p2/306.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer15393367822628749880">Sir William Gerald Golding was a British novelist, poet, and playwright best known for his 1954 novel <a title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies">Lord of the Flies</a>. He was awarded the Booker Prize for literature in 1980 for his novel <a title="Rites of Passage by William Golding" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14431.Rites_of_Passage">Rites of Passage</a>, the first book of the trilogy <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/series/58734-to-the-ends-of-the-earth">To the Ends of the Earth</a>. He was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1983 and was knighted by the Queen of England in 1988.<br /><br />In 2008, <em>The Times</em> ranked Golding</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15393367822628749880">Sir William Gerald Golding was a British novelist, poet, and playwright best known for his 1954 novel <a title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies">Lord of the Flies</a>. He was awarded the Booker Prize for literature in 1980 for his novel <a title="Rites of Passage by William Golding" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14431.Rites_of_Passage">Rites of Passage</a>, the first book of the trilogy <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/series/58734-to-the-ends-of-the-earth">To the Ends of the Earth</a>. He was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1983 and was knighted by the Queen of England in 1988.<br /><br />In 2008, <em>The Times</em> ranked Golding third on their list of "The 50 greatest British writers since 1945".</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15393367822628749880">...more</a>

    </div>
